# Laravel Essential Training

> Laravel, the open-source PHP web framework, has become one of the most widely used ecosystems for building scalable apps at lightning-quick speed. If you’re a PHP developer looking to take your career to the next level, this course was made for you. Join instructor Shruti Balasa as she takes you on a journey through the fundamentals of the Laravel framework.

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** Before you proceed with this course, you should have a good understanding of HTML and should have worked with core PHP before.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=8)** You should also be familiar with object oriented concepts and have some working knowledge of databases like MySQL or SQLite.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=16)** It's also nice to have a good understanding of HTTP requests, but not an absolute requirement. If you know the concepts of MVC architecture, short for Model View Controller, you will be able to pick up Laravel concepts much faster, but even this is not an absolute requirement.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=35)** Apart from these knowledge requirements, I recommend having a dedicated computer or a laptop with a good internet connection for a seamless learning experience.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=45)** You also need a GitHub account to practice building along with the course.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=49)** If you don't have one already, create it before you move further.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=53)** If you're ready, let's get started.

#### Understand the MVC architecture
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=0)** Laravel FrameWork is largely based on the MVC architecture.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=5)** MVC is a way of building a huge application by splitting it into three parts: models, views, and controllers.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=13)** This makes maintaining the application easy as it grows and avoids repetition of code, along with a few other benefits.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=21)** The model contains all the logic related to the application's data, like the schemas, the databases, and their fields.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=30)** This is the part where you write the code to interact with the database.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=35)** The view contains the user interface.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=38)** All the components that the user sees on the web page and interacts with are included in the views.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=44)** The controller is what connects the model and the view.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=48)** It handles all the interactions between these two components.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=51)** So in simpler words, the user interacts with the web page, which is the view.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=57)** The controller takes the user's input from the view, uses some logic to translate that input into a request for the model, the model grabs the data from the database, interacts with it in any other way, and finally, the controller passes this data back from the model to the view for the user to see it.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=77)** Once you have this basic concept in mind during the course, you will better understand why a specific block of code is added in a particular file and not elsewhere.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-the-mvc-architecture?u=76281980&t=87)** Now let's go ahead.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** interface (1), finally, (1), let (1)
> **Env Vars:** mvc (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### Using Codespaces with this course
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=0)** Codespaces is a code editor in the cloud with the full power of Visual Studio Code.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=5)** This entire course is created using GitHub Codespaces, so that you can practice along with me from wherever you are without having to install anything in your system.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=16)** To get started, you can create a Codespace directly from the Course Interface page.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=21)** Click Open next to GitHub Codespaces.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=25)** If you're already logged in to GitHub, you'll be directed to a Getting Started with GitHub Codespaces page.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=32)** If not, you need to log in or create a free GitHub account first.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=36)** On this page, click on Create Codespaces on main button.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=42)** The first time you create a Codespace, it takes several minutes to create the virtual machine and set up everything you need.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=49)** That's what is happening right now.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=52)** Wait until all the setup is done and you see something like this.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=57)** This code editor is a special version of Visual Studio Code running on GitHub servers.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=62)** There are some extensions which are pre-installed for our course.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=66)** You can look at them in this extensions panel.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=69)** The File Explorer here will show you all the files of our project in the current branch.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=75)** We are in the main branch of our repository right now.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=79)** You can see the branch name right here at the bottom.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=82)** Click on it.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=83)** You'll see multiple branches here with names such as 0407, 0304, and so on.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=93)** Each of these branches is associated with one video in the course.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=98)** For example, the branch named 0304 is associated with chapter three video four.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=106)** You'll also see two branches for each video 0304B and 0304E.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=113)** The branch with the suffix B is the beginning state, whereas the one with E is the ending state.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=120)** So if you switch to the branch 0304B, that's exactly the state of the project at the beginning of chapter three, video four and 0304E is the state at the end of the same video.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=134)** So at any point in the course, if you feel lost, you can switch to the corresponding branch and continue from there.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=140)** But there's one thing to note.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=142)** The moment you start a new code space, you will get a unique URL.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=147)** This will become the app URL for Laravel.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=152)** So if you are developing locally on your system, this will always be localhost, so there's no problem.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=157)** But since we're using Codespaces we need to set the asset URL configuration in this file called .env for certain things to work correctly.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=167)** Again, this is automatically taken care of for you. The moment you start a new codespace this line gets added automatically for you.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=178)** Now this was already existing.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=181)** This will be overridden with your asset URL with this URL which is unique to you.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=190)** This project setup is perfect.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=192)** You don't need to change anything, but whenever you try to switch to a new branch, for example, let me try and switch to 0502E.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=201)** You might see an alert like this.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=204)** Your local changes would be overwritten by checkout.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=207)** That's because this asset URL is the local change.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=211)** What do you need to do here is click on Migrate changes.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=215)** Whenever you see an alert like this.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=217)** Leave the stash message blank.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=219)** Hit enter and if you see that there are any merge conflicts that will appear here at the right bottom corner.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=228)** If it does, view the changes and make sure to retain this incoming change.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=234)** This would be called the incoming change and this would be called the original change.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=239)** Accept all the incoming changes to make sure that your asset URL in your .env file is always related to your URL, and then you're good to go.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=251)** Also, while working with Codespaces, if you end up closing the browser window or in my case, I installed the Codespace Chrome app.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=261)** If I end up closing this window, don't worry, your changes will be automatically saved in your GitHub account.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=268)** You can go back to GitHub, Codespaces and open it.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/using-codespaces-with-this-course?u=76281980&t=272)** Continue from wherever you left.

### 1. Laravel Basics: Routes, Views, and Controllers

#### Serve the application
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=0)** Whether you're working with Codespaces or using an IDE of your choice in your local dev environment, this is the starting point of your Laravel application.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=9)** You will see the same set of files and folders in a fresh Laravel app.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=14)** The first thing to do now is to start the development server.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=18)** Open a new terminal within your editor.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=21)** Go to View, Terminal.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=23)** Any more I will use the keyboard shortcut command J to open or close this terminal.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=30)** If you're on windows, the shortcut might be Ctrl+J.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=33)** Now run the command php artisan serve The server is now running.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=41)** You can visit this URL in your browser by pressing the command key on Mac, or Ctrl key on Windows and clicking on it.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=49)** You will see a wonderful splash screen.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=52)** This is a new Laravel application.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=55)** If you scroll to the bottom of the page, you can see which Laravel version is currently installed.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=62)** In my case, it's Version 11.10.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=66)** If you're practicing using Codespaces, you will see the same version.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=70)** But if you install Laravel in your local system, you might see a newer version at the time of installation.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=76)** Certain things might look different if something has changed, but mostly everything will work the same.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=83)** Coming back to the terminal, remember we typed this command.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=87)** PHP Artisan Serve.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=89)** Laravel provides a command line interface called Artisan.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=93)** You can view a list of all the helpful artisan commands by using php artisan list command.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=100)** Click on a new tab, type php artisan list.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=106)** Of course, if you're using Docker and Sail to run Laravel in your local system, you will have to replace PHP in the commands, wherever I type php, you have to replace and type sail instead.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=121)** So this is the huge list of commands that Laravel provides out of the box.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=126)** We will use a lot of these commands, like php artisan migrate, and then make:controller, make:model and many of these.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/serve-the-application?u=76281980&t=138)** Let's go on.

#### Handle routing

#### Building layouts with Blade
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=0)** Blade templates are not only helpful for displaying data and replacing common PHP structures, we can also use them to build and maintain layouts in Laravel.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=11)** Most web applications have the same generic layout across various pages like header, navigation bar, sidebar, and footer.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=19)** Blade provides two methods for creating layouts by reusing code.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=24)** First is by using template inheritance.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=27)** Second is by using blade components.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=30)** Let's look at template inheritance first.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=33)** Consider this web page with a navbar and a main section.

>

>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=344)** Replace the yield directives with simple variables like within curly braces.
>

>

>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=366)** This also has to be the closing x-base tag.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=371)** Let me put it here.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=372)** Notice that we don't even have to say layouts or components or anything, because when we use blade components, all the files, all the components go in here.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=382)** So the moment you prefix it with x- (hyphen) it takes in the name from here.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=389)** This looks very consistent with the markup as compared to the blade directives.
>

>
> **[6:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=400)** So you can say x-slot and use the name attribute to specify the name of this.
>
> **[6:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=408)** So in our case it will be name again.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=410)** And the content of the name goes here.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=413)** This again will be replaced by x-slot.
>
> **[6:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=418)** And here the name is content.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=423)** And this closes with an x-slot closing tag.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=428)** View this in the browser to verify.

> **[7:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=431)** Let's go to the home route and notice that nothing has changed.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/building-layouts-with-blade?u=76281980&t=435)** We can have multiple nested components and multiple slots this way, and this is the most common method followed by Laravel developers right now.

#### Working with controllers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/working-with-controllers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/working-with-controllers?u=76281980&t=0)** Looking at our routes once again for this request, the only action is returning a view.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/working-with-controllers?u=76281980&t=6)** Hence, adding it as a closure makes sense, but this is not always the case.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/working-with-controllers?u=76281980&t=12)** We usually have a lot of logic that goes on for every request.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/working-with-controllers?u=76281980&t=15)** For example, a login request will contain the logic to check if the user's credentials match, to log the user in or to return an error message, and more.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/working-with-controllers?u=76281980&t=26)** We use controllers to group related logic into a single class. By default, controllers are stored within the App\Http\Controllers directory.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/working-with-controllers?u=76281980&t=38)** This is just a default controller created by Laravel for you.

>

### 2. Laravel Basics: Configuration, Database, Migrations, and Eloquent

#### Understand Laravel configuration
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=0)** Every application has a bunch of configuration values that need to be accessed from different files all across the application, like the name of the app, database credentials, mail service credentials, and so on.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=13)** To help manage all these environment variables, Laravel has a .env file at the root of the application.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=21)** These are some app-related variables like name, environment, application key, which is unique to each project, then the app debug value, which determines how much of the error information should be displayed to the user.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=37)** In the local environment, this is set to true by default because the error information is helpful for the developer to debug, but in production, this value should be set to false, otherwise, you risk exposing sensitive data.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=50)** App URL is the base URL. In production, this would contain your domain name.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=56)** Here is a set of database credentials including the type of connection, username, password.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=63)** If you're using version control like git, you need to note that this file should not be committed to your repository.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=70)** You have to add this file to gitignore.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=74)** It's not added here because I'm using Codespaces and the environment file is very much needed.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=80)** But if you install a new Laravel application in your local development environment, you will see that this .env file is already added in gitignore.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=90)** This is not only for security reasons, it's also because each person in your team might have a different set of configuration values and your production server also, which is why we have a .env.example file which has all the variable names similar to .env file without the values or with default values.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=113)** This file is committed to the repository, and everybody on the team can use it to create or edit their own .env files.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=121)** So it's important to make changes to this example file, too, that is, add all of these new variables whenever you add them to your .env file.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=131)** Now, the values of these .env file are retrieved from various files within the config directory.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=139)** Look at database.php, for example.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=143)** The env helper method returns the value of this key from the .env file.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=150)** The second argument value is used as a default value in case the key is not found.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=156)** Now, when we have to access these environment variables, we should not do this directly using the env function.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=163)** Instead, we should use them through the configuration.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=167)** For example, if we wish to access the app name, let's say in our welcome blade view, open welcome.blade.php, and let's say we want to replace this title with our app name.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=181)** You can do so using the config helper method using the dot syntax.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=187)** So our app name will be app.name.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=192)** You can check that right here.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=194)** If you go to app.php, the application name is a variable in the app.php file.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=201)** So that's how you access all of these environment variables using the config method.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=208)** Let's save this file, change our app name here to maybe NewApp, or something like that, and go to the browser, refresh.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=219)** Notice that this has changed.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/understand-laravel-configuration?u=76281980&t=223)** So I encourage you to browse the contents of all the config files here and get a better hold on what are the configurations available in Laravel.

#### Creating and running migrations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=0)** Using the database client extension or any other GUI tool, we can create tables manually. Like you can click on create a new table and so on.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=10)** But in production, creating tables manually is definitely not the best approach.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=15)** Migrations in Laravel help creating and modifying table structures, acting like version control.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=22)** So let's create a new student's table using migrations.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=27)** Open terminal and type the command php artisan make:migration and specify the name of the migration like let's say create_students_table.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=40)** This could be any name, but this particular name will help Laravel understand that you're trying to create a student's table.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=48)** Hit "Enter".
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=49)** The migration is created for us.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=52)** It's within the database directory and migrations.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=57)** We use the schema facade to create and modify tables.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=62)** You can see two methods here, up and down.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=66)** The up method is used to create a table or column, while the down method is used to drop the table or column in case of rollback.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=75)** The create method of schema facade is used for creating a new table which takes in the first parameter as the name of the table, and the closure is used to define the table structure.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=88)** As you can see, Laravel has guessed the table name as students and prefilled it for you.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=92)** It also knows that you want to create it. For specifying the columns, you can see two methods here, ID and timestamps.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=101)** Laravel offers multiple such methods for creating different types of columns.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=105)** The ID method creates the primary key ID column that auto increments, and this timestamps method creates two columns, created_at and updated_at.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=118)** So you don't have to manually type all of these.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=122)** These will be auto-updated.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=124)** Now let's create two columns of our own like name and email.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=129)** So use the string method to create a name column.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=135)** Also, the same method to create an email column as well.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=140)** Okay, let's run this migration now. Open terminal again and type in php artisan migrate.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=149)** Now this command will run all the migrations that haven't previously been run.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=154)** So hit "Enter".
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=155)** Currently, we only have one migration that is new.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=159)** So this is done.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=160)** You should be able to see a student's table now.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=163)** Let's refresh tables.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=165)** And yes, there it is with all the columns that we created.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-and-running-migrations?u=76281980&t=169)** Feel free to explore the structure of the other tables that Laravel created by default, like migrations and password_reset_tokens and so on.

#### Creating models and using Eloquent
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-models-and-using-eloquent?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-models-and-using-eloquent?u=76281980&t=0)** Laravel's Eloquent feature makes it easy and fun to interact with databases.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-models-and-using-eloquent?u=76281980&t=5)** Each table in the database is associated with an Eloquent Model.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-models-and-using-eloquent?u=76281980&t=10)** Through these models, we can retrieve, insert, update, and delete the records from the database.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-models-and-using-eloquent?u=76281980&t=20)** Open the terminal and let's create a model using the command php artisan make:model, followed by the name of the model.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-models-and-using-eloquent?u=76281980&t=31)** By convention, this name should be similar to the table name, but should start with an uppercase and be singular.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/creating-models-and-using-eloquent?u=76281980&t=38)** So in our case, the model name associated with the students table will be Student.
>

>

>

>
> **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=472)** And yes, we get a 419, page expired error.
>
> **[7:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=475)** What does this mean?
>
> **[7:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=477)** This actually happens to prevent cross-site request forgery attacks.
>
> **[8:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=481)** Let's look at the docs.
>
> **[8:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=483)** Go to [laravel.com](https://laravel.com), documentation, and you can search for CSRF.
>
> **[8:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=494)** And you can read more about the vulnerability here.
>
> **[8:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=498)** Basically, all forms that use post, put, patch, or delete requests are vulnerable to such attacks.
>
> **[8:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=506)** So Laravel automatically generates a CSRF token for each session to make sure that the authenticated user is the one making the request and not someone on the outside.
>
> **[8:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=517)** So every form on Laravel with such requests has to include a CSRF token to validate the request, and this can simply be done by adding a CSRF blade directive within the form.
>
> **[8:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=531)** Note that any form with the method post, put, patch, delete has to have the CSRF blade directive, otherwise you get the 419, page expired error.
>
> **[9:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-create-method-and-view-to-show-a-form?u=76281980&t=543)** Now go back and try refreshing the browser.

> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=354)** This is again another security feature of Laravel.

>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=370)** To get around this, open your note model.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=374)** And here, you have two options.
>
> **[6:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=377)** You can either use a protected variable, protected variable fillable.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=385)** And you can specify all the fields that you want to be mass-assigned like we just did.
>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=391)** Or you can use this protected guarded variable and specify the fields that you don't want to be assigned, which you want to protect from mass assignment.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=402)** So here, you can simply use guarded and leave it as a blank array to specify that you don't want any of the fields to be guarded.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=413)** You want all of them to be fillable.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/add-a-store-action-to-save-the-data?u=76281980&t=416)** Just be careful when you do this, because in case you don't want some of the fields to be filled, you'll have to guard them.

> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=169)** We go to the notes table and yes, we do have a uuid.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=173)** Everything was entered perfectly, but if you click on the note to view, you still see the ID itself.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=181)** That is because route model binding uses ID key by default to resolve the model. We can customize this in the model. Open note model.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=193)** Here, add a public method, public function getRouteKeyName() and return uuid here.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=206)** So now any model that you pass using route model binding will be resolved using uuid.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=213)** So now you go back and refresh.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=218)** Let's try opening this note.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=220)** And now great.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/create-a-unique-id-for-each-note?u=76281980&t=222)** You'll see that the primary key in the URL has been successfully replaced with the unique ID for each note.

#### Show flash data to users

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=0)** We don't have any notes right now, so let's quickly create one.

>

>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=21)** But we are not giving user feedback if the action was a success or not.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=26)** In Laravel, this can be done with flashed session data.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=30)** Let's start with the update action.
>

>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=38)** Open the NoteController, and right here in the update action, we're redirecting the user to the show route, but the user has no idea if it was a success or not.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=51)** So here let's pass a success message with success.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=60)** You know, something like changes saved.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=63)** Okay.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=65)** Now, in the show blade view at the top, let's check if we have any session data with success.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=75)** We can do that using @if (session('success')).
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-flash-data-to-users?u=76281980&t=85)** So if we have any data, @endif, then we display this message.
>

### 6. Relationships

#### What are Eloquent relationships?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-are-eloquent-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-are-eloquent-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** Database tables are often related to one another.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-are-eloquent-relationships?u=76281980&t=4)** For example, a post can have many likes, a student can take many subjects, and in our case, a user can have many notes.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-are-eloquent-relationships?u=76281980&t=13)** When you have too many tables and multiple relations, querying with 'where' clauses like get the notes where belongs to user and so on can get tricky and complex.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-are-eloquent-relationships?u=76281980&t=26)** Eloquent makes it easy to work with such relationships and also supports several types of relationships like one to one, one to many, one of many, many to many, and custom types, too.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-are-eloquent-relationships?u=76281980&t=40)** In our case, the user model has a one to many relationship with the note model.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/what-are-eloquent-relationships?u=76281980&t=47)** With this basic knowledge, let's look at how to define Eloquent relationships and then use them to query and save the models.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** case, (2), let (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)

#### Define relationships
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=0)** Once we define relationships in model classes, we can access a related model just like its properties.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=8)** You'll soon understand what I mean.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=10)** Let's begin with the user model.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=12)** Open user.php within app, models.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=17)** To define its relationship with the note model, scroll down to the bottom.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=23)** Add a public method, public function notes, because a user has many notes, and define the relation using
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=38)** $this->hasMany() and pass the note model here, Note::class. And we need to return this.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=50)** So a user has many notes.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=53)** That's what we are trying to define here.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=55)** In case of a one to one relationship, you need to call the hasOne() method instead.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=62)** And you can change this to singular as well.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=65)** Now this definition allows us to access notes of a specific user as if notes were a property of this user model.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=75)** But before we actually do that, let's also define the inverse relationship that lets us access the user of a specific note.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=84)** So open the note model. And here, define public function user.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=93)** Note that this is singular because note belongs to a single user.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=98)** And here we return $this->belongsTo a user.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=105)** Use the User::class here and this is the inverse relationship.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=112)** With this, we can access the user of the note just like any other property of the note model.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/define-relationships?u=76281980&t=119)** You will soon see how useful these relationships are.

#### Solution: Define a new relationship and use it
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=5)** The relation between note and notebook is very similar to that of note and user.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=11)** It's a many-to-one relationship.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=14)** A notebook has many notes and the note belongs to one notebook.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=20)** So first, let's define the hasMany relationship in the notebook model.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=26)** Public function notes.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=28)** This will be plural because a notebook can have many notes. So return $this->hasMany(Note::).
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=40)** Okay, you might have to import the Note::class, app models note.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=45)** Yes.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=46)** Close this.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=47)** Right. Now let's define the inverse relationship in the note model which would be public function notebook.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=57)** This is singular because it's our single notebook. And you can copy this, return $this->belongsTo(Notebook::class). Okay.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=69)** You might have to import if it's not imported.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=72)** Great.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=73)** Now go to the browser.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=75)** And here, we have already added this note into the travel notebook.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=80)** Let's save it once again.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/solution-define-new-relationship-and-use-it?u=76281980&t=82)** So what we need is we need to display the notebook name here right at the top.
>

### 7. Soft Deleting

#### Show soft deleted model
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-soft-deleted-model?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/show-soft-deleted-model?u=76281980&t=0)** We now have the list of trashed items, but if you click on any of the deleted notes, it takes us to the notes route and we get a 404 error because this note is deleted.
>

#### Optional: Set up a local development environment
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/optional-set-up-a-local-development-environment?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/optional-set-up-a-local-development-environment?u=76281980&t=0)** Now, you might want to start using Laravel locally in your system.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/optional-set-up-a-local-development-environment?u=76281980&t=4)** To install Laravel on your system, you first need a local web server like Apache or Nginx, and then you need to install PHP, Composer, and a database server like MySQL, and then configure all of them to work together.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/optional-set-up-a-local-development-environment?u=76281980&t=21)** That's a tedious process where you might run into multiple issues, but thankfully, these days, there are a variety of tools available that help us get started with Laravel within just a few minutes.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/optional-set-up-a-local-development-environment?u=76281980&t=34)** If you go to [laravel.com](https://laravel.com), visit the documentation and go to creating a Laravel project.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/optional-set-up-a-local-development-environment?u=76281980&t=43)** You'll see that the official documentation recommends using Laravel Herd.

#### Quick summary
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=0)** Congratulations!
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=1)** You have built a fully functional, simple notes app using Laravel 11.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=6)** Let's quickly run through all that we learned and created.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=10)** First, we used Laravel Breeze for authentication.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=15)** The authentication routes and views, all of them got created for us.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=19)** Then we created our first model and used migrations.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=23)** We created a resource controller for managing all the CRUD operations on our resource.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=29)** We used Blade templates for our views and created some Blade components.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=34)** We learned about multiple Blade directives.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=36)** We learned how to reuse components as well as entire views based on different routes.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=42)** We used route model binding for injecting an entire model directly into the route, and also learned how to customize this route key to use for resolving the model.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=54)** We then used Eloquent relationships to better manage the relations between database tables while querying and saving the models.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=63)** We then enabled soft deletes on our model to allow for temporary deletion.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=69)** We also learned how to query soft deleted models, restore them, and also permanently destroy them.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=75)** Along the way, we saw some naming conventions, best practices, and more.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/quick-summary?u=76281980&t=81)** I hope all this learning through hands-on project has set a strong foundation for you to dive deeper and build awesome stuff.

> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/next-steps?u=76281980&t=48)** To go deeper into Laravel, you can watch my Advanced Laravel course right here on LinkedIn Learning.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/next-steps?u=76281980&t=55)** You can also refer to the Laracasts forum where a lot of questions have already been asked and answered.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/next-steps?u=76281980&t=62)** Lastly, Laracon, the official Laravel conferences that happen in four different continents is a good place to network with the amazing Laravel community and learn about the latest Laravel news.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/laravel-essential-training/next-steps?u=76281980&t=75)** Don't forget to follow me on Twitter or the X app, @shrutibalasa, and subscribe to my YouTube channel, Thirus.
